    "Eric Talbot Jensen\n\nan emerging principle of customary international law, it would become binding on all States, though it seems unlikely that such a rule has currently reached that status.\n\nThe fundamental rationale for this emerging duty is that a State cannot refuse to monitor in order to avoid “knowing” about cyber activities or to avoid responsibility to prevent harmful cyber activities occurring from within its territory or controlled infrastructure. In other words, State A cannot turn a blind eye to suspected or even potential cyber activities that may cause harm to State B in order to avoid the responsibility to control cyber infrastructure. As early as 1924, a League of Nations Commission of Jurists asked to resolve the Janina-Corfu affair, determined that “if the State has neglected to take all reasonable measures for the prevention of the crime”,[42] that State would be responsible for that harm. Similarly, in the aftermath of the occupation of the US Embassy in Iran, the ICJ held that Iran had failed to take “appropriate steps” to ensure the protection of the US consulate.[43]\n\nThe costs associated with monitoring and preventing cyber harm could be extensive and States may try to avoid those costs and maintain a merely responsive approach. To the extent that this emerging norm binds States, it would not allow such action. Rather, this duty would give States an affirmative responsibility to monitor and prevent.\n\nOne of the difficulties with this emerging duty is that States have different technological capabilities to monitor and prevent cyber activities. The technological capability of a State is a factor to be considered when applying this duty. For example, the standard of care for monitoring and preventing harmful or unlawful cyber activities will be applied more stringently to a technologically developed nation as opposed to an undeveloped nation.[44]\n\nIn assessing the current status under international law of the obligation to prevent acts that unlawfully or adversely affect other States, it is necessary to consider the International Law Commission Draft Articles on Transboundary Harm.[45] The ILC's Draft Articles have not been accepted as a matter of treaty law by States but have been discussed approvingly in multiple international legal fora and quoted as customary international law in numerous situations, including discussions among the General Assembly.\n\nThe International Law Commission concluded in its sixth committee report to the General Assembly that there were four criteria inherent in the definition of transboundary harm: 1) activity in question must be human activity, 2) it must be within the territory or control of one State, 3) it must give rise or be capable of giving rise\n\n42 R. Jennings and A. Watts (eds.), *Oppenheim's International Law*, Vol. I, 9th edition, Part I, p. 549, footnote 4.\n43 US Diplomatic and Consular Staff in Tehran case, para. 63.\n44 ILC Draft Articles on Prevention of Transboundary Harm from Hazardous Activities, commentary to Article 3, para.     "State Obligations in Cyber Operations\n\nto harm, and 4) that harm must be to persons or things within another State.⁴⁶ Therefore, for cyber activities to rise to the level of transboundary harm, they would have to qualify under these criteria. This will be discussed in the following paragraphs.\n\nCyber activities generally meet the requirement of “human activity” because all cyber operations, even if programmed to happen automatically, were initially established by a person, as were the protocols for initiation. There is no obvious reason why cyber activities would not meet this criterion.\n\nWith respect to transboundary harm, the ILC Draft Articles define transboundary harm as harm caused either in another State’s territory or “in other places under the jurisdiction or control of a State other than the State of origin”.⁴⁷ Some scholars have argued that cyberspace should be treated like the commons, therefore out of the sovereign territory of a State.⁴⁸ This argument is unconvincing with respect to this obligation. The hardware and software that compose the World Wide Web exist in physical space that is within some States’ sovereign territory. Even cyber operations from ships on the high seas are located on some ship that is flagged by a certain State and over which that certain State exercises almost exclusive jurisdiction. While it is theoretically possible to envision a set of facts where cyber harm would emanate from an area outside sovereign jurisdiction, the vast majority of cyber incidents in the past and the foreseeable future will be governed by the principles of sovereignty and meet the second criterion.\n\nUnder this rule, cyber harm must affect other States. Transboundary harm is manifested by transboundary physical consequences that result in significant harm to another State’s rights.⁴⁹ While ‘significant’ is not defined in the Draft Articles, the commentary indicates that it is “something more than ‘detectable’ but need not be at the level of ‘serious’ or ‘substantial’”.⁵⁰ Therefore, the harm must lead to a detrimental effect on persons, property, or the environment that may be measured by factual and objective standards.⁵¹\n\n46 D.     "Eric Talbot Jensen\n\nSimilarly, the harm must be “transboundary”. Cyber harms that have purely domestic affects will not invoke this duty. For example, a State has not international legal obligation under this rule to prevent cyber harm that comes from within its own sovereign territory and only has effects in its own sovereign territory. However, international law may be implicated in such circumstances under different rules, such as obligations under human rights.\n\nAs will be discussed later, if a State knows transboundary harm will occur or that there is a risk thereof, it has an obligation to notify and cooperate with the potential victim State. The State of origin is required to consult on preventive or remedial measures after equitably balancing the interest of both States.⁵² The consultation must be done in good faith. The consultation requirement is an on-going obligation and does not cease after an initial consultation.\n\nAnother important consideration of this emerging duty is that the obligation to monitor and prevent harm to another State is one of conduct rather than result.⁵³ Therefore, a State is required “to deploy adequate means, to exercise best possible efforts, to do the utmost”⁵⁴ to prevent or minimise harm to another State. In cases where the State is unable to prevent the harm, State responsibility may accrue, but this duty will not necessarily have been violated. For example, if cyber harm is emanating from within the borders of State A against targets in State B, and State A acts in accordance with this rule to monitor and prevent the harm, State A will have met its duty under international law, even though it still may be liable for damages under the rules of State responsibility.⁵⁵\n\nIn applying this emerging obligation, States cannot be expected to prevent every harm. As discussed above, the principle of *sic utere tuo ut alienum non laedas* assumes that victim States must accept some harm under the doctrine of good neighbourliness.⁵⁶ Therefore, a State is not required to monitor cyber activities with the expectation that it can prevent every harm. Further complicating the analysis, it is unclear what level of harm is an amount necessary to trigger this rule. However, it seems that a standard of reasonableness would apply to these situations.⁵⁷\n\nTo the extent that there is a standard for the State’s duty to monitor and prevent unlawful or harmful activities, it is one of due diligence. As a standard for a State’s duty to prevent transboundary harm, due diligence has a long history and applies to all subjects of international law.⁵⁸ While it was not included as a standard for\n\n⁵² *Ibid.*, Articles 9, 10.\n\n⁵³ *ITLOS Advisory Opinion* (2011), para. 110.\n\n⁵⁴ *Ibid.*\n\n⁵⁵ Responsibility of States for Internationally Wrongful Acts, United Nations, 2001, Ch. Therefore, States must continuously exercise due diligence and continuously consult with potential victim States in order to prevent transboundary harm from cyber activities.⁶⁷\n\nAs with this emerging norm as a whole, the standard of due diligence does not require the achievement of any specific result, but rather that a State takes necessary\n\n59 Commentary to Article 2 of the International Law Commission, Responsibility for Internationally Wrongful Acts, G.A.     "Eric Talbot Jensen\n\nsteps towards achieving that result.⁶⁸ In other words, due diligence requires a State’s conduct to reach a minimum standard of what a “reasonable” or “good” government would do in a specific situation of transboundary harm.⁶⁹\n\nThis was confirmed in an advisory opinion by the International Tribunal for the Law of the Sea where the Tribunal noted that “[t]he sponsoring State’s obligation ‘to ensure’ is not an obligation to achieve ... Rather, it is an obligation to deploy adequate means, to exercise best possible efforts, to do the utmost, to obtain this result to utilize the current technology in international law ... An obligation of ‘conduct’ and not ‘of result’, and as an obligation of ‘due diligence’.”⁷⁰ In the context of cyber activities, exercising due diligence may not prevent cyber harm or unlawful activities, but a State has the legal obligation to “deploy adequate [cyber] means” to monitor potentially harmful cyber activities and prevent the harm from reaching another State.\n\nThe impact of the technological capability of a State mentioned above with respect to this duty also applies to the standard of due diligence.⁷¹ An advanced State may be required to do more by way of due diligence than a State with lesser technological capability.⁷² It will also adjust over time, requiring a State to “keep abreast to technological changes and scientific developments”.⁷³ For example, State A will not be relieved of its due diligence responsibility to monitor and prevent if it simply chooses to not create a government entity to do so out of convenience for the State. Similarly, a State cannot set up an initial cyber monitoring capability and then neglect to update that capability. On the other hand, if a State objectively does not have the financial means to establish or maintain adequate cyber monitoring services (or contract for such services), it may be that the State will have a decreased standard of care in a given situation.\n\nThe due diligence standard also applies to State sponsored commercial activities. International law provides that when a State sponsors a commercial activity, and when that activity has the potential of producing transboundary harm, the sponsoring State must exercise due diligence in preventing such harm.⁷⁴ For example, if a State owns and operates an oil refinery, it must exercise due diligence to ensure that the refinery does not cause transboundary harm.     "State Obligations in Cyber Operations\n\nand operates servers or networks, it must exercise due diligence to make sure those servers and networks do not cause or are not used to cause transboundary harm.\n\nThe principle of due diligence applies not only to State actions, but also to the actions of non-State actors, including its own citizens. “International law imposes the duty upon every state to exercise due diligence to prevent its own subjects, and such foreign subjects as live within its territory, from committing injurious acts against other states.”75 This same notion is found in the Draft Articles on Transboundary Harm which confirms that States have an obligation to apply due diligence to prevent transboundary harm that originates from non-State actors within their territory or over government controlled infrastructure.76 This may require the government to monitor some civilian internet traffic or activity, at least as required to assess potential adverse or unlawful activity. Of course, any such monitoring must comply with both domestic law and international human rights norms.\n\nOn the issue of monitoring private networks, in a State where the majority of internet infrastructure is owned and operated by private companies, the State will have to take some steps to ensure that infrastructure is also properly monitored in order to prevent transboundary harm. It may do this by monitoring the private networks and infrastructure, passing and enforcing laws that require the private companies to monitor and prevent, or some other methods that meet the standard of care of due diligence. As an illustration of this principle, Annex III of UNCLOS provides that “[a] sponsoring state shall not, however, be liable for damage caused by any failure of a contractor sponsored by it to comply with its obligations if that state has adopted laws and regulations ... reasonably appropriate for securing compliance by persons under its jurisdiction”.77\n\nThis same duty of due diligence to monitor networks and prevent transboundary harm applies not only to legitimate private actors but also to criminal elements such as rioters, even though no State responsibility may attach to their actions.78 For example, assume State A discovers that a criminal organisation within its territory is planning harmful cyber activity on a corporation in State B. State A must still exercise due diligence to prevent such activity. The standard to be met by the requirement to exercise due diligence varies with the circumstances, including the target of the harmful activity. Therefore, a possible unlawful cyber act by one private individual against another private individual in another country would require a lesser form of due diligence than a similar event against a significant State asset.79\n\nIn some circumstances, a State may meet its general due diligence requirement by making and enforcing laws that punish wrongdoers and establish the principle of\n\n75 Jennings and Watts, supra note 42, p. 549.\n76 Koivurova, supra note 49; Commentary to UN ILC Draft Articles on Prevention of Transboundary Harm from Hazardous Activities.\n77 UNCLOS Article 4, para. 4.\n78 Jennings and Watts, supra note 42, pp. 550–551.\n79 Ibid., p. 549\n\n85",

However, the principles of transboundary harm are clearly gaining customary status, and there is no reason why they would not implicate cyber activities. Therefore, States should be aware of the progression of the law in this area and take action accordingly.\n\n# 5. Due Regard\n\nIn addition to the due diligence a State must exercise with respect to its own cyber activities they might cause, there is an emerging norm that a State must also act with due regard for the rights of other States in its cyber activities.\n\nThis emerging obligation has its origins in numerous treaties including the 1958 High Seas Convention, $^{81}$  the 1982 United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea, $^{82}$  and the 1967 Outer Space Treaty, $^{83}$  as well as decisions of the International Court of Justice. $^{84}$  The requirement to act with due regard toward the rights of other States is also specifically confirmed with respect to natural resources $^{85}$  and the environment, $^{86}$  including during times of armed conflict. $^{87}$  To the extent that the principle of due\n\n80 Ibid., p. 509.\n81 1958 High Seas Convention.\n82 UNCLOS, Articles 27(4), 39(3)(a), 56(2) 58(3), 60(3), 66(3)(a), 79(5), 87(2), 142(1) 148, 161(4), 162(2)(d), 163(2), 167(2), 234, 267; appendix II, Article 2(1); appendix IV, Articles 51(1)-(2).\n83 Outer Space Treaty, Article IX.\n84 Gabčíkovo-Nagymaros Project, (Hungary/Slovakia), 1997 I.C.J. 7 (25 Sept. 1997) (Judgment).\n85 General Assembly Resolution 1803 on Permanent Sovereignty over Natural Resources, GA Res. 1803 (XVII) / 17 UN GAOR Supp. (No.17) at 15 / UN Doc.     "State Obligations in Cyber Operations\n\nregard becomes customary international law, it would be binding as a standard on all States. There is no evidence that it would apply as a matter of law to non-State actors.\n\nThere is no generally accepted definition of the term due regard. However, one commentator has defined it as an obligation to ensure that the exercise of one State's rights do not cause undue harm to another State's exercise of their rights.⁸⁸ With respect to the high seas, due regard is defined as the obligation to “be aware of and consider the interests of other States in using the high seas, and to refrain from activities that interfere with the exercise by other States of the freedom of the high seas”.⁸⁹ This usage conforms to the term’s general application in other circumstances. Due regard is thought to have two components: 1) an awareness and consideration of either State interests or other factors, and 2) a balancing of those interests and factors when making a decision.⁹⁰\n\nThere is some discussion as to whether this duty applies only to the commons such as the environment and the high seas because most references to due regard involve those situations. However, there are sufficient other agreed applications of the due regard standard to “non-commons” State interests that it seems clear that the obligation to exercise due regard is gaining broader application. For example, when laying submarine cables and pipelines, States must do so with due regard for existing pipelines.⁹¹ Additionally, when making arrests in the territorial sea, the arresting State must show due regard for the other States’ navigational interests.⁹² States must exercise due regard to the rights of other States when exercising their own rights in their exclusive economic zone, and other States must show the same due regard with respect to the rights of the coastal State.⁹³ Aircraft transiting straits must operate with due regard to the safety of navigation.⁹⁴ Numerous similar references illustrate that though many of the due regard obligations must be respected with geographic areas that correspond to the commons, that is certainly not exclusively so and the focus is not the geographic location, but the other State’s interests that might be affected.\n\nA general due regard obligation would require States to exercise due regard for the rights of other States in all of their cyber activities. In other words, when a State takes action regarding cyber activities or cyber incidents, it would need to do so with\n\nManual on International Law Applicable to Armed Conflicts at Sea, 12 June 1994, Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1995, § 35.\n\n88 C. Mendis, ‘Sovereignty vs. Trans-boundary Environmental Harm: The Evolving International Law Obligations and the Sethusamuduram Ship Channel Project’, United Nations (2006) p. 54\n\n89 3 Commentary to the UNCLOS, para. 87.9(1).\n\n90 G.     "Eric Talbot Jensen\n\ndue regard for the rights of other States. For example, when State A wants to establish a cyber security system to protect its infrastructure, it must do so with due regard for the impacts of that system on the rights of other States. So, if the security system would have the effect of slowing or deleting general international cyber traffic, State A may have to find an alternative system or find ways to mitigate the effect on other States.\n\nWith respect to what considerations would be part of the analysis, the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Seas (UNCLOS) commentary states that due regard “implies that all the relevant circumstances are to be taken into consideration”.⁹⁵ This means that when a State exercises due regard, it should take an expansive view of the potential impacts of its actions on the rights of other States. In the cyber context, if State A chooses to shut down servers within its territory as a security measure and knows that this shutdown will impact State B’s internet traffic, State A must exercise due regard for State B when taking their action, including considerations such as State B’s ability to adapt to State A’s actions and State B’s technical capabilities.\n\nThere is no general obligation for a State to exercise due regard with respect to cyber operations that affect only non-State actors. However, special regimes such as the law of armed conflict may have obligations that apply more broadly. Additionally, if State A takes actions that affect a non-State actor in State B, this duty would still apply at least as to State B. In other words, any impacts on State B caused by State A’s actions against the non-State actor in State B would be governed by the duty to act with due regard.\n\nThough the explicit international law with respect to the due regard obligation appears mostly in international environmental and resource law, there are many examples of a more general application and few, if any, examples of States decrying the principle. There are already claims that this principle has, in fact, reached the level of a general principle of customary international law. However, considering all the evidence, this claim seems premature.\n\n## 6. Precautionary Principle\n\nAnother emerging international law principle that has been applied to cyber operations is the precautionary principle.⁹⁶ While some expansion of the application of this norm is certainly taking place, the current law only provides that a State is obliged to apply the precautionary principle with respect to cyber operations that affect the environment.\n\nThis obligation which applies the precautionary principle or approach to cyber activities has its origins in prohibitions against transboundary harm, with particular application to the environment. The modern formulation of the principle is found in\n\n⁹⁵ 4 UNCLOS Commentary, para. 267.3(b).\n⁹⁶ Crawford, supra note 18, p. 357; Schröder, supra note 86.\n\n88",
    "State Obligations in Cyber Operations\n\nthe Stockholm⁹⁷ and Rio Declarations.⁹⁸ It has been incorporated in numerous international agreements.⁹⁹ Though the ICJ has been hesitant to declare it as a customary norm,¹⁰⁰ other tribunals have done so, such as the International Tribunal for the Law of the Sea (ITLOS).¹⁰¹ No major international courts or tribunals have explicitly rejected the principle as customary international law, and recent cases indicate more support for the principle in general.¹⁰² Further, the majority of States participating in international proceedings concerning the principle claim that it is customary international law.¹⁰³ And the majority of scholars confirm the likelihood that it has reached the level of customary international law, at least with respect to the environment.¹⁰⁴\n\nThe principle will at least apply to State parties of various international agreements where the principle has been adopted. It is accepted as customary international law with respect to the environment and is binding on all nations with respect to that discreet area of the law.\n\nLike due regard, the precautionary principle does not have an agreed upon definition. Principle 15 of the Rio Declaration is the most referenced source of the definition and it states: “In order to protect the environment, the precautionary approach shall be widely applied by States according to their capabilities. Where there are threats of serious or irreversible damage, lack of full scientific certainty shall not be used as a reason for postponing cost-effective measures to prevent environmental degradation.”\n\nAnother definition that was formulated in 1988 by a number of scientists, lawyers, policy makers and environmentalists has become known as the “Wingspread Statement on the Precautionary Principle”. It states that “[w]hen an activity raises threats of harm to the environment or human health, precautionary measures\n\n97 Declaration of the United Nations Conference on the Human Environment, Stockholm, June 1972.\n98 Rio Declaration on Environment and Development, Rio de Janiero, June 1992.\n99 World Charter for Nature 1982; Montreal Protocol 1987; Bergen Ministerial Declaration on Sustainable Development in ECE Region 1990; Ministerial Declaration of 3rd International Conference on the Protection of the North Sea; Bamako Convention 1991; 1992 UNECE Convention on the Protection and Use of Transboundary Watercourses and International Lakes; Rio Declaration 1992; UN Framework Convention on Climate Change 1992; Agreement on Application of Sanitary and Phytosanitary Measures 1993; UN Fish Stocks Agreement 1995; Cartagena Protocol on Biosafety; and Stockholm Convention 2000.\n100 See Nuclear Tests case, Judge Weeramantry’s dissenting opinion where he states the precautionary principle is an emerging norm of customary international law.\n101 See Schöder, supra note 86, paras. 119–120.\n102 Nuclear Test cases 1995, Judge Weeramantry’s dissenting opinion, para. 21.\n103 EC-Hormones Case (EU v.     "Eric Talbot Jensen\n\nshould be taken even if some cause and effect relationships are not fully established scientifically\".105\n\nAs a general statement, the precautionary principle involves the process by which policymakers apply science, technology and economics together in order to protect the environment.106 \"Recourse to the precautionary principle presupposes that potentially dangerous effects deriving from a phenomenon, product or process have been identified, and that scientific evaluation does not allow the risk to be determined with sufficient certainty.\"107\n\nThe precautionary principle serves to prevent States from taking unjustified risks that may damage the environment, or on a broader scale, produce transboundary harm. The use of the precautionary principle is \"justified when there is no clear evidence about a particular risk scenario, when the risk itself is uncertain, or until the risk is disproved\".108 In the face of such risks, States must take measures based on the best scientific evidence available. According to the European Union, the measure to be taken should be:\n\nproportional to the chosen level of protection; non-discriminatory in their application; consistent with similar measures already taken; based on an examination of the potential benefits and costs of action or lack of action (including, where appropriate and feasible, an economic cost/benefit analysis); subject to review, in the light of new scientific data, and capable of assigning responsibility for producing the scientific evidence necessary for a more comprehensive risk assessment.109\n\nStates have an obligation to apply the precautionary principle to their cyber operations which may affect the environment. Additionally, there is an increasing number of sources that argue that the precautionary principle is an emerging principle of customary international law that applies to the acts of States more generally.110 The European Commission stated that though the European Union Treaty makes reference to the precautionary principle only with respect to the environment,111 \"in practice, its scope is much wider, and specifically where preliminary objective scientific evaluation, indicates that there are reasonable grounds for concern that the poten\n\n105 ‘Wingspread Conference on the Precautionary Principle’, Science &amp; Environmental Health Network, 26 January 1988, <www.sehn.org wing.html=\"\">, visited on 22 March 2014.\n106 Crawford, supra note 18, p.358.\n107 Communication from the Commission on the Precautionary Principle, COM(2000)1, at 4 (2 February 2000).\n108 Crawford, supra note 18, p. 358.\n109 ‘Communication from the Commission on the Precautionary Principle’, COM(2000)1, at 4 (2 February 2000).\n110 Crawford, supra note 18, p. 358.\n111 EC Treaty, Article 174.</www.sehn.org>",
    "State Obligations in Cyber Operations\n\ntially dangerous effects on the environment, human, animal or plant health may be inconsistent with the high level of protection chosen for the Community”.¹¹²\n\nBecause cyber activities are susceptible to scientific uncertainty and risk, they are likely to be the target of future applications of the precautionary principle. Cyber-attacks and other actions during armed conflict are already subject to the rules on precautions in attack, which include many similar considerations as those included in the precautionary principle. But the application of the precautionary principle to cyber activities more generally would include consideration of factors such as those enunciated by the EU Commission mentioned above. As new cyber tools and techniques are developed, calls for applying the precautionary approach to their use will likely continue.\n\n## 7. International Cooperation\n\nFinally, a State has an obligation to cooperate with a victim State in the event of adverse or unlawful cyber effects from cyber infrastructure located in its territory, or under its exclusive governmental control, that may affect international peace and security. A more general duty to cooperate in response to events that do not threaten international peace and security is emerging, but does not yet have the status of customary international law.\n\nThis obligation is based on UN Charter, Articles 1 and 33.¹¹³ The duty to cooperate has also been affirmed in the ICJ’s *Pulp Mills* case,¹¹⁴ and also has a customary international law basis as pronounced in the UN General Assembly’s Declaration on Principles of International Law concerning Friendly Relations and Co-operation among States in accordance with the Charter of the United Nations¹¹⁵ and other documents such as the UN ILC Draft Articles on Prevention of Transboundary Harm from Hazardous Activities.¹¹⁶\n\nFor the purposes of understanding this legal obligation, cooperation is the “voluntary co-ordinated action of two or more States which takes place under a legal regime and serves a specific objective”.¹¹⁷\n\nIn addition to those circumstances mentioned above where the maintenance of international peace and security is at risk,¹¹⁸ the duty to cooperate also applies to the solving of international problems of economic, social, cultural or humanitar-\n\n¹¹² ‘Communication from the Commission on the Precautionary Principle’, COM(2000)1, at 3 (2 February 2000).\n\n¹¹³ UN Charter, Articles 1, 33.\n\n¹¹⁴ *Pulp Mills* case, *supra* note 41, p. 56.\n\n¹¹⁵ UN GA Declaration on Friendly Relations.\n\n¹¹⁶ UN ILC Draft Articles on Prevention of Transboundary Harm from Hazardous Activities, Article 7.\n\n¹¹⁷ R. Wolfrum, ‘Cooperation, International Law of’, *Max Planck Encyclopedia of Public International Law* (2010).\n\n¹¹⁸ UN Charter, Articles 1(1), 33.",
    "Eric Talbot Jensen\n\nian character;¹¹⁹ the rights and duties of States with respect to Antarctica,¹²⁰ Outer Space¹²¹ and the Seabed,¹²² among others.\n\nThe obligation to cooperate with respect to cyber incidents is enshrined in the European Cyber Crime Convention which has 41 State parties and an additional 11 signatory States. Article 23 requires that “[t]he Parties shall co-operate with each other”¹²³ and provide mutual assistance, particularly with respect to investigations of cyber incidents.¹²⁴ Therefore, for parties to the Convention, the obligation to cooperate is based on the Convention and different that for non-parties.\n\nFor all others, this duty is limited to instances where cyber activities threaten the maintenance of international peace and security. States may become involved in such cyber activities either as host to or victim of cyber activities. In either case, the State has a responsibility to cooperate in resolving the incident peacefully.\n\nIn addition to requirements on the scope of the duty, the law only requires States to cooperate when the adverse or unlawful cyber incident originates from infrastructure within the territory or under its exclusive governmental control or transits the cyber infrastructure in the State’s territory or under its exclusive government control. Both conditions must be met for the duty to be applicable.\n\nNo specific standard for the level of cooperation is clearly agreed upon, but the general consensus is that States must exercise good faith when fulfilling this duty.¹²⁵\n\n## 8. Conclusion\n\nThe law on State obligations with respect to cyber activities is likely to continue to evolve as more and more State practice becomes available. Currently, few obligations have risen to the level of binding as a matter of law. These would certainly include the obligation to resolve certain disputes peacefully as well as the obligation for a State to not let its infrastructure be knowingly uses for activities that will harm another State. The duty to exercise due diligence in monitoring networks and preventing harm seems like a necessary measure to implement that obligation.\n\nThe exact boundaries of other obligations such as exercising due regard for the rights of other States, the application of the precautionary principle, and the duty to cooperate are still developing. The passage of time and the actions of States will undoubtedly influence that development.\n\n119 UN Charter, Article 1(3).\n120 Antarctica Treaty, Articles 2, 3.\n121 Treaty on Principles Governing the Activities of States in the Exploration and Use of Outer Space, Including the Moon and Other Celestial Bodies, 27 Jan. 1967, 18 U.S.T. 2410, T.I.A.S.
